Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) tend to result in a high yield. This is likely due to the ability of these groups to coordinate with the metal centers, enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a high yield. The phenyl group may enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst, while the hydroxyl group can participate in hydrogen bonding, stabilizing the structure. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The sulfanyl group can form strong bonds with metal centers, potentially enhancing the catalytic activity. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring tend to result in a low yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine ring may coordinate too strongly with the metal center, potentially inhibiting the desired reactivity. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple methoxy groups (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups might disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may introduce steric hindrance or unfavorable electronic effects that reduce catalytic efficiency. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a nitro group (–NO2) tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group may enhance the electrophilicity of the metal center, promoting reactivity. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (–CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing effect of the trifluoromethyl group may overly stabilize the metal center, reducing its reactivity. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole group can participate in π-π stacking and hydrogen bonding, potentially enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

**End of Rules**

    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) tend to result in a high yield. These groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability and reactivity. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a high yield. The phenyl group may enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst, while the hydroxyl group can participate in hydrogen bonding, stabilizing the structure. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The sulfanyl group can form strong bonds with metal centers, potentially enhancing catalytic activity. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring tend to result in a low yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine ring may coordinate too strongly with the metal center, potentially inhibiting desired reactivity. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple methoxy groups (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups might disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may introduce steric hindrance or unfavorable electronic effects that reduce catalytic efficiency. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a nitro group (–NO2) tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group may enhance the electrophilicity of the metal center, promoting reactivity. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (–CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing effect of the trifluoromethyl group may overly stabilize the metal center, reducing its reactivity. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole group can participate in π-π stacking and hydrogen bonding, potentially enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran structure can facilitate π-π interactions and possibly stabilize the metal center. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with an alkyl chain of 7 or more carbons tend to result in a low yield. The increased steric bulk may hinder effective interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a bromine atom (–Br) tend to result in a high yield. The bromine atom may facilitate radical formation, enhancing the reaction process. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a low yield. The combination of these groups may lead to unfavorable interactions with the catalyst. (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a low yield. The strong coordination of the pyridine nitrogen with the metal center may inhibit reactivity. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a methoxy group (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating methoxy group may disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid)

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) with an alkyl chain of 5 or fewer carbons tend to result in a high yield. These groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability and reactivity. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a high yield. The phenyl group may enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst, while the hydroxyl group can participate in hydrogen bonding, stabilizing the structure. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The sulfanyl group can form strong bonds with metal centers, potentially enhancing catalytic activity. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a low yield. The strong coordination of the pyridine nitrogen with the metal center may inhibit reactivity. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple methoxy groups (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups might disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a low yield. The combination of these groups may lead to unfavorable interactions with the catalyst. (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a nitro group (–NO2) tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group may enhance the electrophilicity of the metal center, promoting reactivity. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (–CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing effect of the trifluoromethyl group may overly stabilize the metal center, reducing its reactivity. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole group can participate in π-π stacking and hydrogen bonding, potentially enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran structure can facilitate π-π interactions and possibly stabilize the metal center. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with an alkyl chain of 7 or more carbons tend to result in a low yield. The increased steric bulk may hinder effective interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a bromine atom (–Br) tend to result in a high yield. The bromine atom may facilitate radical formation, enhancing the reaction process. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a methoxy group (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating methoxy group may disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring tend to result in a low yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine ring may coordinate too strongly with the metal center, potentially inhibiting desired reactivity. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may introduce steric hindrance or unfavorable electronic effects that reduce catalytic efficiency. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a bromine atom (–Br) tend to result in a high yield. The bromine atom may facilitate radical formation, enhancing the reaction process. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a methoxy group (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating methoxy group may disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring tend to result in a low yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine ring may coordinate too strongly with the metal center, potentially inhibiting desired reactivity. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may introduce steric hindrance or unfavorable electronic effects that reduce catalytic efficiency. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current validation accuracy of 0.6 is significantly lower than the previous reference accuracies, which were consistently around 0.76. This suggests that the current rules may not be as effective in capturing the underlying patterns in the data.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The drop in validation accuracy indicates potential underfitting, where the model is not capturing the complexity of the data. The rules might be too simplistic or not comprehensive enough to account for the variability in the dataset.

3. **Discrepancy Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, rules with high negative SHAP values are likely problematic. For instance, Rule 1 consistently shows negative SHAP values for these cases, indicating it might be contributing to underestimation.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, rules with high positive SHAP values should be scrutinized. Rule 4, for example, shows a high positive SHAP value in some cases, suggesting it might be overestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvement Interpretation:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest that there is room for improvement. The significant drop in accuracy compared to previous iterations indicates that the current rules may not be capturing the necessary features effectively.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights specific rules that may need refinement. For instance, Rule 1 and Rule 4 have shown significant SHAP values, indicating their strong influence on predictions. Adjusting these rules could potentially improve model performance.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rules with High Positive SHAP Values:**
   - Rules like Rule 4, which show high positive SHAP values for overestimated loadings, should be revisited. Consider modifying the conditions or adding additional constraints to better align predictions with experimental values.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**
   - Rules such as Rule 1, which have high negative SHAP values for underestimated loadings, may need to be expanded or adjusted to capture more relevant features that contribute to high loading.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - To address potential underfitting, consider incorporating additional features or interactions that might be missing from the current rules. This could involve exploring new chemical groups or structural motifs that influence catalyst loading.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules that consistently show high SHAP values, as these have the most significant impact on predictions. Test modifications in a controlled manner to evaluate their effect on model performance.

5. **Incorporate Domain Knowledge:**
   - Leverage domain expertise to identify potential chemical interactions or structural features that are not currently captured by the rules. This could provide new avenues for rule development and improve the model's ability to generalize across different datasets.

By addressing these areas, the rules can be refined to better capture the complexity of the data, potentially leading to improved model performance and more accurate predictions.

    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) with an alkyl chain of 5 or fewer carbons tend to result in a high yield. These groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability and reactivity. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a high yield. The phenyl group may enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst, while the hydroxyl group can participate in hydrogen bonding, stabilizing the structure. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The sulfanyl group can form strong bonds with metal centers, potentially enhancing catalytic activity. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a low yield. The strong coordination of the pyridine nitrogen with the metal center may inhibit reactivity. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple methoxy groups (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups might disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a nitro group (–NO2) tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group may enhance the electrophilicity of the metal center, promoting reactivity. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (–CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing effect of the trifluoromethyl group may overly stabilize the metal center, reducing its reactivity. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole group can participate in π-π stacking and hydrogen bonding, potentially enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran structure can facilitate π-π interactions and possibly stabilize the metal center. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an alkyl chain of 7 or more carbons tend to result in a low yield. The increased steric bulk may hinder effective interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a bromine atom (–Br) tend to result in a high yield. The bromine atom may facilitate radical formation, enhancing the reaction process. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a methoxy group (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating methoxy group may disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring tend to result in a low yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine ring may coordinate too strongly with the metal center, potentially inhibiting desired reactivity. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may introduce steric hindrance or unfavorable electronic effects that reduce catalytic efficiency. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance metal coordination and stabilize the catalyst through hydrogen bonding and thiol-metal interactions. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) with an alkyl chain of 5 or fewer carbons tend to result in a high yield. These groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability and reactivity. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a high yield. The phenyl group may enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst, while the hydroxyl group can participate in hydrogen bonding, stabilizing the structure. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The sulfanyl group can form strong bonds with metal centers, potentially enhancing catalytic activity. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a low yield. The strong coordination of the pyridine nitrogen with the metal center may inhibit reactivity. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple methoxy groups (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups might disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a nitro group (–NO2) tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group may enhance the electrophilicity of the metal center, promoting reactivity. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (–CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing effect of the trifluoromethyl group may overly stabilize the metal center, reducing its reactivity. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole group can participate in π-π stacking and hydrogen bonding, potentially enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran structure can facilitate π-π interactions and possibly stabilize the metal center. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an alkyl chain of 7 or more carbons tend to result in a low yield. The increased steric bulk may hinder effective interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a bromine atom (–Br) tend to result in a high yield. The bromine atom may facilitate radical formation, enhancing the reaction process. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a methoxy group (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating methoxy group may disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring tend to result in a low yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine ring may coordinate too strongly with the metal center, potentially inhibiting desired reactivity. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may introduce steric hindrance or unfavorable electronic effects that reduce catalytic efficiency. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance metal coordination and stabilize the catalyst through hydrogen bonding and thiol-metal interactions. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

ML Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules seem to be struggling with accurately predicting the loading of modifiers on catalysts, as evidenced by the validation accuracy of 0.6, which is lower than the previous reference accuracies (0.76). This suggests that the rules may not be capturing the necessary features or interactions effectively.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting:**
   - The consistent validation accuracy of 0.6 across iterations suggests potential underfitting. The model may not be complex enough to capture the underlying patterns in the data, or the rules may not be sufficiently detailed or accurate.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, rules with high positive SHAP values (e.g., Rule 1) may be overestimating the effect of certain features.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, rules with high negative SHAP values (e.g., Rule 4) may be underestimating the effect of certain features.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest limited improvement potential with the existing rules. The model's performance has not improved significantly across iterations, indicating that the rules may need substantial refinement or expansion.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis indicates that certain rules (e.g., Rule 1 and Rule 4) have a significant impact on predictions. These rules may need to be refined to better align with experimental outcomes.
   - The presence of multiple rules with negative SHAP values for underestimated loadings suggests that these rules may be too restrictive or not capturing important interactions.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Overestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 1**: Consider revising the conditions under which a high yield is predicted. The presence of both a carboxylic acid and an amino group may not always lead to high loading, especially if other structural features are present.
   - **Rule 4**: Re-evaluate the impact of the pyridine ring and carboxylic acid group. The strong coordination of pyridine nitrogen may not always inhibit reactivity as expected.

2. **Adjust Underestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 4**: Since it has a high negative SHAP value for underestimated loadings, consider relaxing the conditions or incorporating additional features that may contribute to high loading.
   - **Rule 6**: The nitro group's effect may be more context-dependent than currently accounted for. Consider interactions with other functional groups.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce additional rules or modify existing ones to capture more complex interactions between functional groups and the catalyst.
   - Consider incorporating machine learning techniques to identify patterns or interactions that are not currently captured by the rules.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively test and refine rules. Focus on rules with the most significant SHAP values and adjust them based on experimental discrepancies.
   - Continuously evaluate the impact of rule changes on validation accuracy to ensure improvements are being made.

5. **Expand Feature Set:**
   - Consider expanding the feature set used to generate the feature matrix. This could include additional chemical properties or interactions that may influence catalyst loading.

By addressing these areas, the rules can be refined to improve the model's accuracy and better align predictions with experimental results.

Project Manager Message:
### Summary of Discussions

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules are not effectively predicting the loading of modifiers on catalysts, as indicated by a validation accuracy of 0.6, which is lower than previous reference accuracies.
   - The rules may not be capturing necessary features or interactions effectively, suggesting potential underfitting.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting:**
   - The consistent validation accuracy across iterations suggests underfitting, indicating that the model or rules may not be complex enough to capture the underlying patterns in the data.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - Overestimated predictions (high predicted loading, low experimental value) may be due to rules like Rule 1 overestimating certain features.
   - Underestimated predictions (low predicted loading, high experimental value) may be due to rules like Rule 4 underestimating certain features.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest limited improvement potential with existing rules, indicating a need for substantial refinement or expansion.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights significant impacts from certain rules, suggesting a need for refinement to better align with experimental outcomes.
   - Negative SHAP values for underestimated loadings suggest that some rules may be too restrictive.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Overestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 1**: Reassess the conditions for high yield predictions, considering additional structural features that may influence outcomes.
   - **Rule 4**: Re-evaluate the impact of pyridine rings and carboxylic acid groups, as their coordination may not always inhibit reactivity.

2. **Adjust Underestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 4**: Relax conditions or incorporate additional features to better capture high loading scenarios.
   - **Rule 6**: Consider context-dependent interactions of the nitro group with other functional groups.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce new rules or modify existing ones to capture complex interactions between functional groups and the catalyst.
   - Use machine learning techniques to identify patterns not currently captured by the rules.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use SHAP analysis insights to iteratively test and refine rules, focusing on those with significant SHAP values.

A lift greater than 1 indicates that the rule is useful and that the presence of the condition increases the likelihood of the outcome. The current lift suggests that the rules are effectively identifying conditions that are more likely to lead to the desired outcome compared to random chance.

3. **Leverage**: The current train leverage is 0.0075, which is a positive value, indicating that the rules provide additional support over what would be expected by chance. Although the leverage is not very high, it is still an improvement over the previous negative test leverage values, which indicated weaker associations.

Overall, the current rules show improved performance in terms of confidence and lift compared to the previous metrics. The increase in confidence and lift suggests that the rules are becoming more effective at identifying conditions that lead to high yields.

- **Incorporate More Data**: If possible, incorporate more data to validate the rules and ensure that they generalize well across different datasets.
- **Analyze Outliers**: Investigate any outliers or exceptions where the rules do not perform as expected. Understanding these cases can provide insights into potential improvements or additional conditions to consider.
- **Iterative Testing**: Continue iterative testing and validation to ensure that the rules maintain high confidence and lift across different datasets and conditions.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting:**
   - The consistent validation accuracy of 0.6 suggests potential underfitting. The model may not be complex enough or the rules may not be capturing the necessary features to differentiate between high and low loading effectively. The lack of improvement across iterations indicates that the model is not learning effectively from the data.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 often has a high positive SHAP value, indicating it might be overestimating the loading. Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 1 also frequently has a high negative SHAP value, suggesting it might be underestimating the loading. This inconsistency suggests that Rule 1 may need refinement to better capture the nuances of the data.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics do not suggest a high probability of improvement with the existing rules. The lack of variation in validation accuracy across iterations indicates that the model may have reached a local minimum, and further iterations with the current rules may not yield significant improvements.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - The SHAP analysis highlights Rule 1 as a significant contributor to both overestimated and underestimated loadings. This suggests that Rule 1 may be too broad or not specific enough, leading to inconsistent predictions. Additionally, Rule 4 and Rule 13 also show significant SHAP values in some cases, indicating potential areas for refinement.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rule 1:**
   - Given its significant impact on SHAP values, Rule 1 should be refined to better capture the specific conditions under which a high loading is expected. Consider incorporating additional features or conditions that may influence the loading, such as steric effects or specific electronic interactions.

2. **Adjust Rule 4 and Rule 13:**
   - These rules also show significant SHAP values in some cases. Consider refining these rules to better account for the specific conditions under which they apply. For example, Rule 4 could be adjusted to consider the presence of additional electron-withdrawing groups more explicitly.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - To address potential underfitting, consider expanding the feature set or increasing the complexity of the model. This could involve incorporating additional chemical descriptors or using more advanced machine learning techniques to capture complex interactions.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules with high SHAP values for both overestimated and underestimated loadings, and adjust them to better capture the nuances of the data. This iterative process should help improve the model's ability to generalize and accurately predict loadings.

5. **Evaluate New Rules:**
   - After refining the rules, evaluate their effectiveness using cross-validation and compare the results to the baseline metrics. This will help determine if the changes have led to improvements in model performance.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) with an alkyl chain of 5 or fewer carbons tend to result in a high yield. Additionally, consider the electronic effects of branching in the alkyl chain, which may enhance metal coordination. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a high yield. The phenyl group may enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst, while the hydroxyl group can participate in hydrogen bonding, stabilizing the structure. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The sulfanyl group can form strong bonds with metal centers, potentially enhancing catalytic activity. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a low yield unless additional electron-withdrawing groups are present, which can mitigate the strong coordination effect. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple methoxy groups (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups might disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a nitro group (–NO2) tend to result in a high yield, especially when combined with other electron-withdrawing groups, which can enhance the electrophilicity of the metal center. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (–CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing effect of the trifluoromethyl group may overly stabilize the metal center, reducing its reactivity. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole group can participate in π-π stacking and hydrogen bonding, potentially enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran structure can facilitate π-π interactions and possibly stabilize the metal center. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an alkyl chain of 7 or more carbons tend to result in a low yield. The increased steric bulk may hinder effective interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a bromine atom (–Br) tend to result in a high yield. The bromine atom may facilitate radical formation, enhancing the reaction process. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a methoxy group (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating methoxy group may disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring tend to result in a low yield unless paired with electron-withdrawing groups that can balance the strong coordination effect. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may introduce steric hindrance or unfavorable electronic effects that reduce catalytic efficiency. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance metal coordination and stabilize the catalyst through hydrogen bonding and thiol-metal interactions. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

The rules might be too simplistic or not comprehensive enough to cover the variability in the data.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) with an alkyl chain of 5 or fewer carbons tend to result in a high yield. This combination may enhance metal coordination through chelation. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a high yield. The phenyl group can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst, while the hydroxyl group can participate in hydrogen bonding, stabilizing the structure. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The sulfanyl group can form strong bonds with metal centers, potentially enhancing catalytic activity. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a low yield unless additional electron-withdrawing groups are present, which can mitigate the strong coordination effect. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple methoxy groups (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups might disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a nitro group (–NO2) tend to result in a high yield, especially when combined with other electron-withdrawing groups, which can enhance the electrophilicity of the metal center. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (–CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing effect of the trifluoromethyl group may overly stabilize the metal center, reducing its reactivity. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole group can participate in π-π stacking and hydrogen bonding, potentially enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran structure can facilitate π-π interactions and possibly stabilize the metal center. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an alkyl chain of 7 or more carbons tend to result in a low yield. The increased steric bulk may hinder effective interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a bromine atom (–Br) tend to result in a high yield. The bromine atom may facilitate radical formation, enhancing the reaction process. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a methoxy group (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating methoxy group may disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring tend to result in a low yield unless paired with electron-withdrawing groups that can balance the strong coordination effect. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may introduce steric hindrance or unfavorable electronic effects that reduce catalytic efficiency. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance metal coordination and stabilize the catalyst through hydrogen bonding and thiol-metal interactions. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) with an alkyl chain of 5 or fewer carbons tend to result in a high yield, unless they also contain bulky groups or strong electron-withdrawing groups, which may reduce yield. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a high yield. The phenyl group can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst, while the hydroxyl group can participate in hydrogen bonding, stabilizing the structure. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The sulfanyl group can form strong bonds with metal centers, potentially enhancing catalytic activity. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a low yield unless additional electron-withdrawing groups are present, which can mitigate the strong coordination effect. However, the presence of electron-donating groups or steric hindrance can further reduce yield. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple methoxy groups (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups might disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a nitro group (–NO2) tend to result in a high yield, especially when combined with other electron-withdrawing groups, which can enhance the electrophilicity of the metal center. The position and number of nitro groups can further influence the yield. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (–CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing effect of the trifluoromethyl group may overly stabilize the metal center, reducing its reactivity. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole group can participate in π-π stacking and hydrogen bonding, potentially enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran structure can facilitate π-π interactions and possibly stabilize the metal center. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an alkyl chain of 7 or more carbons tend to result in a low yield. The increased steric bulk may hinder effective interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a bromine atom (–Br) tend to result in a high yield. The bromine atom may facilitate radical formation, enhancing the reaction process. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a methoxy group (–OCH3) tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating methoxy group may disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring tend to result in a low yield unless paired with electron-withdrawing groups that can balance the strong coordination effect. The presence of additional electron-donating groups can further reduce yield. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may introduce steric hindrance or unfavorable electronic effects that reduce catalytic efficiency. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance metal coordination and stabilize the catalyst through hydrogen bonding and thiol-metal interactions. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) with an alkyl chain of 5 or fewer carbons tend to result in a high yield, unless they also contain bulky groups like tert-butyl or strong electron-withdrawing groups such as trifluoromethyl, which may reduce yield. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) at the para position tend to result in a high yield. The para position allows optimal π-π interactions and hydrogen bonding, stabilizing the structure. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a high yield. This combination enhances metal coordination and stabilizes the catalyst through hydrogen bonding and thiol-metal interactions. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a low yield unless paired with electron-withdrawing groups like nitro (–NO2) or cyano (–CN) that can mitigate the strong coordination effect. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple electron-donating groups such as methoxy (–OCH3) or amino (–NH2) tend to result in a low yield. These groups can disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a nitro group (–NO2) at the para position tend to result in a high yield, especially when combined with other electron-withdrawing groups, enhancing the electrophilicity of the metal center. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (–CF3) tend to result in a low yield unless combined with groups that can donate electrons, such as amino groups, to balance the electron-withdrawing effect. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole group can participate in π-π stacking and hydrogen bonding, enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran structure facilitates π-π interactions and possibly stabilizes the metal center. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an alkyl chain of 7 or more carbons tend to result in a low yield due to increased steric bulk hindering effective interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a bromine atom (–Br) tend to result in a high yield. The bromine atom may facilitate radical formation, enhancing the reaction process. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may introduce steric hindrance or unfavorable electronic effects that reduce catalytic efficiency. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring tend to result in a low yield unless paired with electron-withdrawing groups that can balance the strong coordination effect. The presence of additional electron-donating groups can further reduce yield. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance metal coordination and stabilize the catalyst through hydrogen bonding and thiol-metal interactions. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (–NO2) and trifluoromethyl (–CF3) tend to result in a high yield by enhancing the electrophilicity of the metal center. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

**End of Rules**

Final Metric Message:

    Target Name: yield
    GPT Model: gpt-4o-2024-08-06
    ML Model: ETC
    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) with an alkyl chain of 5 or fewer carbons tend to result in a high yield, unless they also contain bulky groups like tert-butyl or strong electron-withdrawing groups such as trifluoromethyl, which may reduce yield. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) at the para position tend to result in a high yield. The para position allows optimal π-π interactions and hydrogen bonding, stabilizing the structure. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a high yield. This combination enhances metal coordination and stabilizes the catalyst through hydrogen bonding and thiol-metal interactions. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a low yield unless paired with electron-withdrawing groups like nitro (–NO2) or cyano (–CN) that can mitigate the strong coordination effect. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple electron-donating groups such as methoxy (–OCH3) or amino (–NH2) tend to result in a low yield. These groups can disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a nitro group (–NO2) at the para position tend to result in a high yield, especially when combined with other electron-withdrawing groups, enhancing the electrophilicity of the metal center. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (–CF3) tend to result in a low yield unless combined with groups that can donate electrons, such as amino groups, to balance the electron-withdrawing effect. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole group can participate in π-π stacking and hydrogen bonding, enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran structure facilitates π-π interactions and possibly stabilizes the metal center. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an alkyl chain of 7 or more carbons tend to result in a low yield due to increased steric bulk hindering effective interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a bromine atom (–Br) tend to result in a high yield. The bromine atom may facilitate radical formation, enhancing the reaction process. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may introduce steric hindrance or unfavorable electronic effects that reduce catalytic efficiency. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring tend to result in a low yield unless paired with electron-withdrawing groups that can balance the strong coordination effect. The presence of additional electron-donating groups can further reduce yield. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance metal coordination and stabilize the catalyst through hydrogen bonding and thiol-metal interactions. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (–NO2) and trifluoromethyl (–CF3) tend to result in a high yield by enhancing the electrophilicity of the metal center. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) with an alkyl chain of 5 or fewer carbons tend to result in a high yield, unless they also contain bulky groups like tert-butyl or strong electron-withdrawing groups such as trifluoromethyl, which may reduce yield. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) at the para position tend to result in a high yield. The para position allows optimal π-π interactions and hydrogen bonding, stabilizing the structure. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a high yield. This combination enhances metal coordination and stabilizes the catalyst through hydrogen bonding and thiol-metal interactions. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a low yield unless paired with electron-withdrawing groups like nitro (–NO2) or cyano (–CN) that can mitigate the strong coordination effect. The effectiveness of these groups is enhanced when they are positioned ortho or para to the pyridine ring. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid)

ML Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules seem to be struggling with effectiveness, as indicated by the validation accuracy of 0.5428571428571428, which is lower than the previous reference accuracies (0.76 consistently). This suggests that the rules may not be capturing the underlying patterns in the data effectively.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting:**
   - The consistent validation accuracy of 0.6 across multiple iterations suggests potential underfitting. The model may not be complex enough to capture the nuances of the data, or the rules may not be adequately tailored to the dataset.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 often has a high positive SHAP value, indicating it might be overestimating the loading.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 1 also frequently has a high negative SHAP value, suggesting it might be underestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics do not suggest significant improvement over previous iterations. The validation accuracy has decreased compared to the reference metrics, indicating that the current rules may not be optimal.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis indicates that Rule 1 is frequently involved in both overestimations and underestimations, suggesting it may need refinement.
   - Rules with high negative SHAP values, such as Rule 7 and Rule 9, in cases of underestimated loadings, may need adjustments to better capture the conditions leading to high experimental loadings.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rule 1:**
   - Rule 1 appears to be a significant contributor to both overestimations and underestimations. Consider breaking it down into more specific sub-rules that account for different structural features or interactions that could influence loading.

2. **Adjust Rule 7 and Rule 9:**
   - These rules have high negative SHAP values in cases of underestimated loadings. Re-evaluate the conditions under which these rules predict low yield and consider incorporating additional structural features or interactions that could lead to high yield.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - To address potential underfitting, consider introducing more complex rules or additional features that capture the nuances of the dataset. This could involve incorporating more detailed chemical interactions or environmental factors.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules that consistently show high SHAP values (positive or negative) in mispredicted cases and adjust them to better align with the experimental data.

5. **Incorporate Additional Data:**
   - If possible, incorporate additional data or features that could provide more context or information about the modifiers and their interactions with the catalyst. This could help improve the model's ability to generalize and make accurate predictions. 

By focusing on these areas, the rules can be refined to better capture the underlying patterns in the data, potentially leading to improved accuracy and more reliable predictions.

   - The current rules are not performing optimally, as indicated by a validation accuracy of 0.5428571428571428, which is lower than previous reference accuracies. This suggests that the rules may not be effectively capturing the underlying patterns in the data.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting:**
   - The consistent validation accuracy of 0.6 across multiple iterations suggests potential underfitting. The model may not be complex enough to capture the nuances of the data, or the rules may not be adequately tailored to the dataset.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - Rule 1 is frequently involved in both overestimations and underestimations, indicating it might need refinement.
   - Rules 7 and 9 have high negative SHAP values in cases of underestimated loadings, suggesting they may need adjustments to better capture conditions leading to high experimental loadings.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics do not suggest significant improvement over previous iterations. The validation accuracy has decreased compared to the reference metrics, indicating that the current rules may not be optimal.

   - Rules with high negative SHAP values, such as Rule 7 and Rule 9, in cases of underestimated loadings, may need adjustments to better capture the conditions leading to high experimental loadings.

2. **Adjust Rule 7 and Rule 9:**

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce more complex rules or additional features that capture the nuances of the dataset. This could involve incorporating more detailed chemical interactions or environmental factors.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules that consistently show high SHAP values (positive or negative) in mispredicted cases and adjust them to better align with the experimental data.

5. **Incorporate Additional Data:**
   - If possible, incorporate additional data or features that could provide more context or information about the modifiers and their interactions with the catalyst. This could help improve the model's ability to generalize and make accurate predictions.

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) with an alkyl chain of 5 or fewer carbons tend to result in a high yield. This is due to the ability of these groups to facilitate coordination with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group (–C6H5) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) at the para position tend to result in a high yield. The para position allows optimal π-π interactions and hydrogen bonding, stabilizing the structure. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a hydroxyl group (–OH) tend to result in a high yield. This combination enhances metal coordination and stabilizes the catalyst through hydrogen bonding and thiol-metal interactions. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a low yield unless paired with electron-withdrawing groups like nitro (–NO2) or cyano (–CN) that can mitigate the strong coordination effect. The effectiveness of these groups is enhanced when they are positioned ortho or para to the pyridine ring. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple electron-donating groups such as methoxy (–OCH3) or amino (–NH2) tend to result in a low yield. These groups can disrupt the electronic environment of the catalyst. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a nitro group (–NO2) at the para position tend to result in a high yield, especially when combined with other electron-withdrawing groups, enhancing the electrophilicity of the metal center. The presence of additional stabilizing groups like hydroxyl (–OH) can further enhance this effect. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (–CF3) tend to result in a low yield unless combined with groups that can donate electrons, such as amino groups, to balance the electron-withdrawing effect. The presence of additional electron-withdrawing groups can further reduce yield. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole group can participate in π-π stacking and hydrogen bonding, enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuran structure facilitates π-π interactions and possibly stabilizes the metal center. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an alkyl chain of 7 or more carbons tend to result in a low yield due to increased steric bulk hindering effective interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a bromine atom (–Br) tend to result in a high yield. The bromine atom may facilitate radical formation, enhancing the reaction process. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group may introduce steric hindrance or unfavorable electronic effects that reduce catalytic efficiency. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring tend to result in a low yield unless paired with electron-withdrawing groups that can balance the strong coordination effect. The presence of additional electron-donating groups can further reduce yield. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and a sulfanyl group (–SH) tend to result in a high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance metal coordination and stabilize the catalyst through hydrogen bonding and thiol-metal interactions. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (–NO2) and trifluoromethyl (–CF3) tend to result in a high yield by enhancing the electrophilicity of the metal center. The presence of additional stabilizing groups like hydroxyl (–OH) can further enhance this effect. (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid)
